23/04/2025, 03:21 - Priyanshu: Bhai?
23/04/2025, 14:33 - Priyanshu: Good morning everyone,

Today I‚Äôll be introducing you to the principle of superconducting levitation, a fascinating phenomenon rooted in the unique properties of superconductors.

Let‚Äôs start with the basics: What is Superconductivity?

Superconductivity is a quantum state of matter where certain materials, when cooled below a specific temperature called the critical temperature, exhibit two remarkable properties:
First, they have absolutely zero electrical resistance. This means that once an electric current starts flowing in a superconductor, it can continue indefinitely without any energy loss.
Second, superconductors expel magnetic fields from their interior‚Äîa property known as the Meissner effect.

This breakthrough was first discovered by Heike Kamerlingh Onnes in 1911, as shown in the image on the slide. The graph here demonstrates how the resistance of mercury suddenly drops to zero at around 4.2 Kelvin, marking the onset of superconductivity.

Superconductors can be classified based on how they respond to external magnetic fields:

Type I superconductors completely expel magnetic fields up to a certain limit.

Type II superconductors allow magnetic fields to partially penetrate through them in quantized tubes, leading to more complex behavior, especially in strong magnetic fields.

Now, let‚Äôs connect this to Magnetic Levitation.

Magnetic levitation is the process of suspending an object in air using magnetic forces. Superconducting levitation is a special type of magnetic levitation that occurs when a magnet is suspended above a superconductor.

This remarkable effect is made possible by two key phenomena:

The Meissner effect, which causes the superconductor to repel magnetic fields, creating a lifting force.

Flux pinning, which occurs in Type II superconductors. Here, magnetic field lines get trapped or ‚Äòpinned‚Äô at defects within the material, locking the magnet in place and providing stability.

The image on the slide shows an example of this: a supercooled superconductor, likely chilled with liquid nitrogen, stably levitating a magnet above it. The visible vapor is from the cooling process.

Together, the Meissner effect and flux pinning create stable, frictionless levitation, which is not only visually impressive but also has practical applications in technologies like maglev trains and frictionless bearings.
